## Chapter: Textbook for Theory of Solids I

### Introduction

Welcome to the fourteenth chapter of "Textbook for Theory of Solids I". In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of solid state physics. This branch of physics deals with the study of the physical properties of solid materials, such as metals, semiconductors, and insulators. Solid state physics is a fundamental field of study that has a wide range of applications, from electronics and materials science to nanotechnology and renewable energy.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles and theories that govern the behavior of solids. We will start by discussing the crystal structure of solids and how it affects their physical properties. We will then move on to study the electronic structure of solids, including the band theory and the concept of energy bands. We will also cover topics such as conductivity, magnetism, and superconductivity.

One of the key concepts in solid state physics is the idea of a lattice, which is a regular arrangement of atoms or molecules in a solid material. We will discuss the different types of lattices and their properties, as well as the effects of lattice defects on the behavior of solids. We will also touch upon the concept of phonons, which are quantized lattice vibrations that play a crucial role in the thermal and mechanical properties of solids.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical equations and models to explain the various phenomena in solid state physics. It is important to note that these equations are not just abstract concepts, but they have been experimentally verified and have practical applications in various fields.

In conclusion, this chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of solid state physics, laying the foundation for further studies in this field. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of solids!


## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 14.1 Electrical Properties of Solids:

In this section, we will explore the electrical properties of solids, specifically focusing on electrical conductivity and resistivity. These properties are essential in understanding the behavior of solids and have significant applications in various fields, such as electronics and materials science.

#### 14.1a Electrical Conductivity and Resistivity

Electrical resistivity ($\rho$) is a fundamental property of a material that measures its resistance to electric current. It is defined as the ratio of the electric field ($E$) to the current density ($J$) in a material, given by the equation:

$$
\rho = \frac{E}{J}
$$

The SI unit of electrical resistivity is ohm-metre ($\Omega \cdot m$). A low resistivity indicates a material that readily allows electric current to pass through it, while a high resistivity indicates a material that resists the flow of electric current.

On the other hand, electrical conductivity ($\sigma$) is the reciprocal of electrical resistivity and represents a material's ability to conduct electric current. It is given by the equation:

$$
\sigma = \frac{1}{\rho}
$$

The SI unit of electrical conductivity is siemens per metre (S/m). Conductivity and resistivity are intensive properties of materials, meaning they are independent of the size or shape of the material.

In solids, the electrical resistivity and conductivity are primarily determined by the properties of the material's crystal structure and the movement of electrons within the material. In metals, the electrons are delocalized and can move freely, resulting in high conductivity. In contrast, in insulators, the electrons are tightly bound to the atoms, leading to low conductivity.

The electrical resistivity of a material can also be affected by external factors such as temperature, pressure, and impurities. For example, in semiconductors, the addition of impurities can significantly alter the material's conductivity, making it suitable for use in electronic devices.

In conclusion, the electrical conductivity and resistivity of solids play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of materials and have significant applications in various fields. In the following sections, we will explore these properties in more detail and understand their relationship with other physical properties of solids.


#### 14.1b Semiconductors and Insulators

Semiconductors and insulators are two types of materials that have distinct electrical properties. In this subsection, we will explore the differences between these two materials and their significance in solid state physics.

Semiconductors are materials that have an electrical conductivity between that of conductors and insulators. They have a band gap, which is the energy difference between the highest occupied energy level (valence band) and the lowest unoccupied energy level (conduction band). In semiconductors, the band gap is small enough that thermal energy can excite electrons from the valence band to the conduction band, allowing for some degree of electrical conductivity.

One of the key characteristics of semiconductors is their ability to be doped, meaning impurities can be intentionally added to the material to alter its electrical properties. This process can either increase or decrease the material's conductivity, making semiconductors highly versatile in various applications. For example, doping silicon with boron or phosphorus can create p-type or n-type semiconductors, respectively, which are essential in the production of electronic devices such as transistors and diodes.

On the other hand, insulators are materials that have a large band gap, making it difficult for electrons to move from the valence band to the conduction band. As a result, insulators have very low electrical conductivity. This property makes them useful in applications where electrical insulation is necessary, such as in power lines and electronic circuit boards.

The electrical properties of semiconductors and insulators are crucial in understanding the behavior of these materials in various environments. For example, the band gap of a semiconductor can be affected by temperature, pressure, and impurities, which can significantly impact its electrical conductivity. Additionally, the band gap also plays a role in determining the optical properties of these materials, such as their ability to absorb or emit light.

In conclusion, semiconductors and insulators are two types of materials with distinct electrical properties that have significant applications in various fields, including electronics and materials science. Understanding the behavior of these materials is essential in the study of solid state physics and the development of new technologies. 


#### 14.1c Superconductivity

Superconductivity is a phenomenon in which certain materials exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a critical temperature. This unique property has been observed in a variety of materials, including metals, alloys, and even some organic compounds. In this subsection, we will explore the electrical properties of superconductors and their significance in solid state physics.

The discovery of superconductivity in molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) monolayers by Marvin L. Cohen has opened up new possibilities for studying and understanding this phenomenon in low-dimensional systems. Under an electric field, MoS2 monolayers have been found to superconduct at temperatures below 9.4 K, providing a new platform for investigating the effects of dimensionality on superconductivity.

In his book "Superconductivity in low-carrier-density systems: Degenerate semiconductors," Cohen discusses the role of electron-phonon interactions in superconductivity and how they can be affected by the carrier density of a material. This is particularly relevant in low-carrier-density systems, such as degenerate semiconductors, where the electron-phonon coupling can be enhanced, leading to higher critical temperatures for superconductivity.

Cohen's work on fitting pseudopotentials to experimental data and their subsequent application, as discussed in his book chapter "The fitting of pseudopotentials to experimental data and their subsequent application," has also been instrumental in understanding the electronic structure of superconductors. By accurately modeling the electronic properties of materials, Cohen and his colleagues have been able to predict the occurrence of superconductivity in various systems.

The theory of short-range order and disorder in tetrahedrally bonded semiconductors, as discussed in Cohen's book chapter "Theory of short-range order and disorder in tetrahedrally bonded semiconductors," has also been applied to the study of superconductivity. This theory provides a framework for understanding the effects of impurities and defects on the electronic properties of materials, which can have a significant impact on the occurrence and behavior of superconductivity.

In his book "Electronic Structure and Optical Properties of Semiconductors," Cohen and his colleague J. R. Chelikowsky provide a comprehensive overview of the electronic structure of semiconductors and how it relates to their optical properties. This understanding is crucial in the study of superconductivity, as the optical properties of a material can provide valuable insights into its electronic structure and behavior.

The emergence of superconductivity in condensed matter physics has also been a topic of interest for Cohen. In his book chapter "Emergence in condensed matter physics," he discusses how the collective behavior of electrons in a material can give rise to new phenomena, such as superconductivity. This concept of emergence is essential in understanding the complex behavior of superconductors and their potential applications.

In conclusion, the study of superconductivity has greatly benefited from the contributions of Marvin L. Cohen and his colleagues. Through their work on electronic structure, impurity effects, and emergent behavior in materials, we have gained a deeper understanding of this fascinating phenomenon. As we continue to explore the electrical properties of solids, superconductivity will undoubtedly remain a topic of great interest and importance in the field of solid state physics.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 14.2 Magnetic Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.2a Diamagnetism and Paramagnetism

In the previous section, we discussed the phenomenon of superconductivity and its significance in solid state physics. In this section, we will explore another important aspect of solid state physics - the magnetic properties of solids.

Magnetism is a fundamental property of matter that arises from the spin and orbital motion of electrons. In solids, the magnetic properties are determined by the arrangement of electrons in the material and their interactions with each other. There are three main types of magnetism observed in solids: diamagnetism, paramagnetism, and ferromagnetism.

Diamagnetism is the weakest form of magnetism and is observed in all materials. It arises from the orbital motion of electrons, which creates a small magnetic field that opposes an external magnetic field. This results in a slight repulsion between the material and the external magnetic field. Diamagnetism is a universal property of matter and is not affected by temperature or the presence of other magnetic materials.

On the other hand, paramagnetism is a much stronger form of magnetism and is observed in materials that contain unpaired electrons. These unpaired electrons have a magnetic moment, which aligns with an external magnetic field, resulting in a net attraction between the material and the external field. The strength of paramagnetism is directly proportional to the number of unpaired electrons in the material.

Examples of paramagnetic materials include transition metals, rare earth elements, and some organic compounds. In these materials, the unpaired electrons arise from incomplete electron shells or the presence of impurities. The magnetic susceptibility of paramagnetic materials follows the Curie or Curie-Weiss laws, which describe the relationship between the magnetic susceptibility and temperature.

The Curie law states that the magnetic susceptibility is inversely proportional to the temperature, while the Curie-Weiss law takes into account the interactions between the magnetic moments of the electrons. In principle, any system that contains unpaired electrons can be called a paramagnet, but the interactions between the electrons must be carefully considered.

In systems with minimal interactions, such as a dilute gas of monatomic hydrogen atoms, the only pure paramagnet is observed. However, in most materials, the interactions between the electrons result in a mixed system, where both diamagnetic and paramagnetic contributions are present. For example, in a gas of lithium atoms, the diamagnetic contribution from the paired core electrons is dominant, but the paramagnetic contribution from the unpaired valence electrons is also present.

The quenching of magnetic moments due to the pairing of electrons is a common phenomenon in many elements. For instance, hydrogen, which has one unpaired electron in its ground state, is diamagnetic due to the pairing of electrons in the molecular form (H2). Similarly, in heavier elements, the diamagnetic contribution becomes more significant, and in metallic gold, it dominates the magnetic properties.

In conclusion, the magnetic properties of solids play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of materials at the atomic level. Diamagnetism and paramagnetism are two fundamental types of magnetism observed in solids, and their study has led to significant advancements in the field of solid state physics. In the next section, we will explore another type of magnetism - ferromagnetism - and its role in the properties of solids.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 14.2 Magnetic Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.2b Ferromagnetism and Antiferromagnetism

In the previous section, we discussed the two weakest forms of magnetism - diamagnetism and paramagnetism. In this section, we will explore the two strongest forms of magnetism observed in solids - ferromagnetism and antiferromagnetism.

Ferromagnetism is the strongest form of magnetism and is observed in materials that contain unpaired electrons with aligned spins. This alignment of spins creates a net magnetic moment, resulting in a strong attraction between the material and an external magnetic field. Unlike paramagnetism, ferromagnetism can exist even in the absence of an external magnetic field. This is due to the presence of domains, which are regions within the material where the magnetic moments of the electrons are aligned in the same direction. These domains can be aligned in different directions, resulting in a net magnetization of zero. However, when an external magnetic field is applied, the domains align in the direction of the field, resulting in a strong magnetization.

Examples of ferromagnetic materials include iron, cobalt, and nickel. These materials have partially filled electron shells, resulting in unpaired electrons with aligned spins. The strength of ferromagnetism is directly proportional to the number of unpaired electrons in the material.

On the other hand, antiferromagnetism is a type of magnetism that arises from the interaction between neighboring atoms in a crystal lattice. In this case, the magnetic moments of the electrons are aligned in opposite directions, resulting in a net magnetization of zero. This type of magnetism is observed in materials with a crystal structure that does not allow for a single ground state, leading to frustration. This frustration can arise from geometric factors, such as in the Kagome lattice, or from disorder in the material.

One example of antiferromagnetism is synthetic antiferromagnets, which are artificial structures consisting of two or more thin ferromagnetic layers separated by a nonmagnetic layer. The dipole coupling between the layers results in antiparallel alignment of the magnetization, making them useful in applications such as giant magnetoresistance.

In conclusion, the magnetic properties of solids are determined by the arrangement of electrons and their interactions with each other. Diamagnetism and paramagnetism are the weakest forms of magnetism, while ferromagnetism and antiferromagnetism are the strongest. These different types of magnetism play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of materials and have important applications in various fields, such as data storage and medical imaging. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 14.2 Magnetic Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.2c Magnetic Domains and Hysteresis

In the previous section, we discussed the two strongest forms of magnetism observed in solids - ferromagnetism and antiferromagnetism. In this section, we will explore the concept of magnetic domains and hysteresis, which play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of ferromagnetic materials.

# Magnetic Domains

As mentioned in the previous section, ferromagnetic materials contain domains, which are regions within the material where the magnetic moments of the electrons are aligned in the same direction. These domains can be aligned in different directions, resulting in a net magnetization of zero. However, when an external magnetic field is applied, the domains align in the direction of the field, resulting in a strong magnetization.

The size of these domains is determined by the balance of several energies within the material. Each time a region of magnetization splits into two domains, it creates a domain wall between the domains, where magnetic dipoles (molecules) with magnetization pointing in different directions are adjacent. The exchange interaction, which creates the magnetization, is a force that tends to align nearby dipoles so they point in the same direction. Forcing adjacent dipoles to point in different directions requires energy. Therefore, a domain wall requires extra energy, called the domain wall energy, which is proportional to the area of the wall.

The net amount that the energy is reduced when a domain splits is equal to the difference between the magnetic field energy saved and the additional energy required to create the domain wall. The field energy is proportional to the cube of the domain size, while the domain wall energy is proportional to the square of the domain size. As the domains get smaller, the net energy saved by splitting decreases. The domains keep dividing into smaller domains until the energy cost of creating an additional domain wall is just equal to the field energy saved. Then the domains of this size are stable. In most materials, the domains are microscopic in size, around 10<sup>−4</sup> - 10<sup>−6</sup> m.

# Hysteresis

When a ferromagnetic material is subjected to an external magnetic field, it exhibits a phenomenon known as hysteresis. This refers to the lag in the response of the material to changes in the external magnetic field. This lag is due to the presence of magnetic domains within the material.

When an external magnetic field is applied, the domains align in the direction of the field, resulting in a strong magnetization. However, when the external field is removed, the domains do not immediately return to their original state. Instead, they remain aligned in the direction of the previous field, resulting in a residual magnetization. This is known as the remanent magnetization.

To reverse the magnetization, an external field in the opposite direction must be applied. This field is known as the coercive field. Once the coercive field is reached, the domains start to realign in the opposite direction, and the magnetization decreases until it reaches zero. This process is known as magnetic saturation.

The hysteresis loop, which plots the relationship between the external magnetic field and the magnetization, is a characteristic of ferromagnetic materials. The shape and size of the loop depend on the properties of the material, such as the strength of the exchange interaction and the number of unpaired electrons.

# Conclusion

In this section, we have explored the concept of magnetic domains and hysteresis, which play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of ferromagnetic materials. The size of magnetic domains is determined by the balance of energies within the material, and the presence of these domains leads to the phenomenon of hysteresis. Understanding these concepts is essential in the study of solid state physics and the applications of ferromagnetic materials in technology.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 14.3 Optical Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.3a Light Absorption and Reflection

In the previous section, we discussed the magnetic properties of solids, specifically focusing on ferromagnetism and antiferromagnetism. In this section, we will shift our focus to the optical properties of solids, specifically light absorption and reflection.

# Light Absorption and Reflection

When light interacts with a solid material, it can either be absorbed or reflected. The amount of light absorbed or reflected depends on the properties of the material, such as its composition, structure, and electronic properties. In this subsection, we will explore the mechanisms behind light absorption and reflection in solids.

## Absorption

When light is incident on a solid material, it can be absorbed by the material's electrons. This absorption occurs when the energy of the incident light matches the energy difference between two electronic states in the material. The absorbed energy is then converted into thermal energy, causing the material to heat up.

The amount of light absorbed by a material is determined by its absorption coefficient, which is a measure of how easily a material absorbs light. The absorption coefficient is dependent on the material's composition and structure, as well as the wavelength of the incident light. Materials with a high absorption coefficient will absorb more light, while materials with a low absorption coefficient will reflect more light.

## Reflection

When light is incident on a solid material, it can also be reflected off the material's surface. The amount of light reflected depends on the material's reflectivity, which is a measure of how well a material reflects light. The reflectivity of a material is dependent on its composition, structure, and the angle of incidence of the light.

One important phenomenon related to reflection is Brewster's angle. This is the angle at which light with a specific polarization (known as p-polarization) is completely transmitted through a material, with no reflection. This angle is dependent on the refractive index of the material and can be used to determine the refractive index of a material.

## Pseudo-Brewster's Angle

In some cases, when the reflecting surface is absorbing, the reflectivity at p-polarization goes through a non-zero minimum at an angle known as the pseudo-Brewster's angle. This angle is similar to Brewster's angle, but it takes into account the absorption of the material. This phenomenon is important in understanding the optical properties of materials with absorbing surfaces.

# Multivariate Optical Element

In recent years, a new technology known as multivariate optical computing has emerged, which utilizes the principles of multivariate analysis to analyze the composition of materials. This technology makes use of a key component called a multivariate optical element (MOE).

An MOE is a specially designed optical element that is tuned to a specific pattern or composition of a material. When light is incident on the MOE, it interacts with the material and produces a unique spectral pattern. This pattern can then be analyzed using multivariate statistics to determine the composition of the material.

Compared to traditional spectrometry techniques, multivariate optical computing offers faster and more efficient analysis of materials. It also has the potential to achieve the same level of accuracy as laboratory-grade spectroscopic systems. This technology has a wide range of applications, from analyzing the composition of powder mixtures to detecting impurities in pharmaceuticals.

In conclusion, the optical properties of solids play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of materials. By understanding the mechanisms behind light absorption and reflection, as well as the advancements in technologies such as multivariate optical computing, we can gain a deeper understanding of the composition and structure of solids. 


### Section: 14.3 Optical Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.3b Refractive Index and Dispersion

In the previous subsection, we discussed the mechanisms behind light absorption and reflection in solids. In this subsection, we will explore the refractive index and dispersion of solids, which are important optical properties that affect the behavior of light in materials.

## Refractive Index

The refractive index of a material is a measure of how much the speed of light is reduced when it passes through the material. It is defined as the ratio of the speed of light in a vacuum to the speed of light in the material. The refractive index is dependent on the material's composition, structure, and the wavelength of the incident light.

When light passes from one material to another with a different refractive index, it changes direction. This phenomenon is known as refraction and is responsible for the bending of light when it passes through a lens or a prism. The amount of refraction depends on the difference in refractive index between the two materials.

## Dispersion

Dispersion is the phenomenon where the refractive index of a material varies with the wavelength of the incident light. This means that different wavelengths of light will experience different amounts of refraction when passing through the material. This effect is responsible for the splitting of white light into its component colors when passing through a prism.

The amount of dispersion in a material is determined by its dispersion coefficient, which is a measure of how much the refractive index changes with wavelength. Materials with a high dispersion coefficient will exhibit a larger difference in refractive index between different wavelengths, resulting in a more pronounced splitting of light.

## Relationship between Refractive Index and Dispersion

The refractive index and dispersion of a material are closely related. In general, materials with a higher refractive index will also exhibit a higher dispersion coefficient. This means that materials with a high refractive index will cause a greater separation of colors when light passes through them, resulting in a more pronounced rainbow effect.

However, there are exceptions to this relationship. Some materials, known as anomalous dispersive materials, have a high refractive index but a low dispersion coefficient. This means that they will cause a large amount of refraction, but the difference in refractive index between different wavelengths will be small. These materials are often used in optical devices to minimize the dispersion of light.

## Applications of Refractive Index and Dispersion

The refractive index and dispersion of materials play a crucial role in many optical devices and technologies. For example, in fiber optics, the refractive index of the core material is carefully chosen to ensure that light is efficiently transmitted through the fiber. In lenses and prisms, the dispersion of the material is used to manipulate the colors of light for various applications, such as in cameras and telescopes.

In addition, the refractive index and dispersion of materials are also important in the study of solid state physics. By understanding how light interacts with materials, we can gain insights into the electronic and structural properties of solids. This knowledge is crucial for the development of new materials and technologies in the field of solid state physics.

## Conclusion

In this subsection, we have explored the refractive index and dispersion of solids, two important optical properties that affect the behavior of light in materials. We have seen how these properties are related and how they play a crucial role in various applications and in the study of solid state physics. In the next subsection, we will continue our discussion on the optical properties of solids by exploring the phenomenon of polarization.


### Section: 14.3 Optical Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 14.3c Photonic Crystals and Metamaterials

In the previous subsection, we discussed the refractive index and dispersion of solids, which are important optical properties that affect the behavior of light in materials. In this subsection, we will explore the unique optical properties of photonic crystals and metamaterials, which are engineered materials that exhibit extraordinary optical properties.

## Photonic Crystals

Photonic crystals are periodic structures that have a refractive index that varies periodically in space. This periodic variation in refractive index creates a band gap, which is a range of frequencies where light cannot propagate through the material. This band gap is similar to the electronic band gap in semiconductors, but instead of affecting the motion of electrons, it affects the propagation of light.

The band gap in photonic crystals can be tuned by changing the periodicity of the structure or the refractive index of the material. This allows for control over the wavelengths of light that can pass through the material, making photonic crystals useful for applications such as optical filters and waveguides.

## Metamaterials

Metamaterials are artificially engineered materials that exhibit properties not found in natural materials. They are typically composed of subwavelength structures that are arranged in a periodic pattern. This unique structure gives metamaterials their extraordinary optical properties, such as negative refractive index and superlensing.

One of the most well-known classes of metamaterials is the split-ring resonator (SRR) metamaterial, which consists of an array of split-ring resonators that exhibit a negative refractive index. This negative refractive index allows for the creation of a perfect lens, which can focus light beyond the diffraction limit.

## Relationship between Photonic Crystals and Metamaterials

Photonic crystals and metamaterials share some similarities in their periodic structures, but they also have distinct differences. While photonic crystals rely on the periodicity of the structure to create a band gap, metamaterials use the subwavelength structure to achieve unique optical properties.

Additionally, photonic crystals are typically made from natural materials, while metamaterials are engineered from artificial materials. This allows for greater control over the optical properties of metamaterials, making them ideal for applications such as cloaking devices and perfect lenses.

In conclusion, photonic crystals and metamaterials are two types of engineered materials that exhibit extraordinary optical properties. Their unique structures and properties make them valuable tools for controlling and manipulating light, and they continue to be an active area of research in the field of solid state physics.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of solid state physics. We have learned about the properties of solids, including their structure, bonding, and electronic properties. We have also discussed the various types of solids, such as metals, semiconductors, and insulators, and how their properties differ. Additionally, we have delved into the behavior of electrons in solids, including their energy levels and band structures. Finally, we have examined the effects of temperature and external forces on solids, and how they can alter their properties.

Solid state physics is a vast and complex field, and this chapter has only scratched the surface. There is still much to be explored and discovered in this area of study. As we continue to advance our understanding of solids, we will be able to develop new materials with unique properties and applications. The knowledge gained from solid state physics has already led to numerous technological advancements, and it will continue to do so in the future.

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of quantum mechanics as it applies to solids. Quantum mechanics is a fundamental theory that describes the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level. It has revolutionized our understanding of the physical world and has had a profound impact on many fields, including solid state physics.

We will begin by exploring the basic principles of quantum mechanics, including the wave-particle duality and the uncertainty principle. We will then move on to discuss the quantum mechanical description of solids, which is based on the concept of energy bands. We will see how the behavior of electrons in a solid can be described using the Schrödinger equation and how this leads to the formation of energy bands.

Next, we will examine the concept of band structure and how it relates to the electronic properties of solids. We will also discuss the role of crystal symmetry in determining the band structure and how it affects the electronic properties of solids.

Finally, we will explore some of the key phenomena that arise from the quantum mechanical nature of solids, such as superconductivity and the quantum Hall effect. These phenomena have important technological applications and have been the subject of intense research in recent years.

Overall, this chapter will provide a solid foundation in the quantum mechanics of solids, which is essential for understanding the electronic properties of materials and their applications in various fields. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of quantum mechanics of solids.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 15.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 15.1a Quantum Numbers and Quantum States

In the previous chapter, we discussed the basic principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level. In this chapter, we will explore how these principles can be applied to the study of solids.

One of the key concepts in quantum mechanics is the idea of quantum states. These states describe the possible energy levels that a particle can occupy. In the case of solids, the particles we are interested in are electrons. Just like in the case of atoms, electrons in solids can only occupy certain energy levels, or quantum states.

The quantum numbers <math>n</math>, <math>\ell</math>, and <math>m</math> are used to describe these quantum states. These numbers are integers and can have specific values, as shown in the related context. The quantum number <math>n</math> represents the principal quantum number and determines the energy level of the electron. The quantum number <math>\ell</math>, also known as the azimuthal quantum number, describes the shape of the electron's orbital. The quantum number <math>m</math>, or magnetic quantum number, determines the orientation of the orbital in space.

It is important to note that these quantum numbers are restricted by certain rules. For example, <math>\ell</math> can only have values from 0 to <math>n-1</math>, and <math>m</math> can only have values from <math>-l</math> to <math>l</math>. These restrictions are in accordance with the Heisenberg uncertainty principle, which states that the position and momentum of a particle cannot be known simultaneously with certainty.

The angular momentum of an electron in a solid is also quantized, just like in the case of atoms. The angular momentum operator, <math>\hat{L}</math>, has eigenvalues given by <math>\hbar^2 \ell(\ell+1)</math>, where <math>\hbar</math> is the reduced Planck's constant. This means that the angular momentum of an electron in a solid can only take on certain discrete values.

The projection of the angular momentum onto a specific direction, <math>\hat{L}_z</math>, is also quantized and has eigenvalues given by <math>\hbar m</math>. This means that the component of the angular momentum along a specific direction can only take on certain values, as described by the quantum number <math>m</math>.

It is important to note that the total angular momentum of an electron in a solid is not just determined by its orbital angular momentum, but also by its spin. The spin of an electron is a fundamental property that cannot be explained by classical physics. It is quantized and can have two possible values, <math>\pm \frac{1}{2}</math>. This means that the total angular momentum of an electron in a solid is given by the sum of its orbital angular momentum and its spin.

In summary, the quantum numbers and quantum states play a crucial role in describing the behavior of electrons in solids. They determine the energy levels and angular momentum of the electrons, which in turn affect the electronic properties of materials. In the next section, we will explore how these quantum states give rise to energy bands in solids.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 15.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 15.1b Pauli Exclusion Principle

In the previous section, we discussed the quantum numbers and quantum states that describe the energy levels and properties of electrons in solids. However, there is a fundamental principle that governs the behavior of these particles and their quantum states: the Pauli exclusion principle.

The Pauli exclusion principle, formulated by Austrian physicist Wolfgang Pauli in 1925, states that two or more identical particles with half-integer spins (i.e. fermions) cannot occupy the same quantum state within a quantum system simultaneously. This principle was later extended to all fermions with Pauli's spin-statistics theorem in 1940.

In the case of electrons in solids, this principle can be stated as follows: it is impossible for two electrons of a poly-electron atom to have the same values of the four quantum numbers: "n", the principal quantum number; "<ell>", the azimuthal quantum number; "m<sub><ell></sub>", the magnetic quantum number; and "m<sub>s</sub>", the spin quantum number. This means that if two electrons reside in the same orbital, then their "n", "<ell>", and "m<sub><ell></sub>" values are the same; therefore their "m<sub>s</sub>" must be different, and thus the electrons must have opposite half-integer spin projections of 1/2 and −1/2.

This principle has important implications for the behavior of electrons in solids. It explains why electrons in atoms and molecules are arranged in specific energy levels and why certain energy levels can only hold a certain number of electrons. It also plays a crucial role in determining the electronic and magnetic properties of materials.

It is important to note that the Pauli exclusion principle only applies to fermions, particles with half-integer spin. Particles with an integer spin, or bosons, are not subject to this principle and can occupy the same quantum state. This is why, for example, photons produced by a laser or atoms in a Bose-Einstein condensate can all occupy the same quantum state.

A more rigorous statement of the Pauli exclusion principle is that, concerning the exchange of two identical particles, the total (many-particle) wave function is antisymmetric for fermions, and symmetric for bosons. This means that if the space "and" spin coordinates of two identical particles are interchanged, then the total wave function changes its sign for fermions and does not change for bosons.

In summary, the Pauli exclusion principle is a fundamental principle in quantum mechanics that governs the behavior of fermions, including electrons in solids. It explains the arrangement of electrons in atoms and molecules and plays a crucial role in determining the properties of materials. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 15.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 15.1c Fermi Energy and Fermi Surface

In the previous section, we discussed the quantum numbers and quantum states that describe the energy levels and properties of electrons in solids. However, there is another important concept that arises from the quantum nature of electrons in solids: the Fermi energy.

The Fermi energy, named after Italian physicist Enrico Fermi, is a fundamental property of a quantum system that describes the highest energy state occupied by electrons at absolute zero temperature. It is a crucial concept in understanding the electronic properties of materials, particularly metals.

The Fermi energy is defined as the energy of the highest occupied state in a system of non-interacting fermions, such as electrons in a metal. In other words, it is the energy at which the probability of finding an electron is 50% in a system at absolute zero temperature. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$E_\text{F} = \frac{\hbar^2}{2m_0} \left( \frac{3 \pi^2 N}{V} \right)^{2/3}$$

where "N" is the number of particles, "m"<sub>0</sub> the rest mass of each fermion, "V" the volume of the system, and <math>\hbar</math> the reduced Planck constant.

The Fermi energy is a crucial quantity in understanding the electronic properties of metals. Under the free electron model, the electrons in a metal can be considered to form a Fermi gas. The number density <math>N/V</math> of conduction electrons in metals ranges between approximately 10<sup>28</sup> and 10<sup>29</sup> electrons/m<sup>3</sup>, which is also the typical density of atoms in ordinary solid matter. This number density produces a Fermi energy of the order of 2 to 10 electronvolts.

One of the most important consequences of the Fermi energy is the formation of the Fermi surface. The Fermi surface is a 3-dimensional surface in momentum space that separates the occupied and unoccupied states at the Fermi energy. It is a crucial concept in understanding the electrical and thermal conductivity of metals, as well as their magnetic and optical properties.

The shape and size of the Fermi surface depend on the crystal structure and electronic band structure of the material. In metals, the Fermi surface is typically a complex shape, with multiple branches and pockets. In contrast, insulators and semiconductors have a closed Fermi surface, as their electrons are confined to the valence and conduction bands, respectively.

In conclusion, the Fermi energy and Fermi surface are fundamental concepts in the quantum mechanics of solids. They play a crucial role in understanding the electronic properties of materials, particularly metals, and are essential for predicting and explaining their behavior. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 15.2 Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 15.2a Quantum Tunneling Phenomenon

Quantum tunneling is a fundamental phenomenon in quantum mechanics that plays a crucial role in the behavior of particles in solids. In this section, we will explore the concept of quantum tunneling in solids and its implications for the electronic properties of materials.

#### 15.2a Quantum Tunneling Phenomenon

Quantum tunneling is the quantum mechanical phenomenon where a particle can pass through a potential barrier, even if it does not have enough energy to overcome the barrier in classical mechanics. This phenomenon arises from the wave-like nature of particles, where they can exist in a superposition of states and have a finite probability of being found in a region that is classically forbidden.

In solids, quantum tunneling is particularly relevant for electrons, which are the primary carriers of charge and heat in materials. The behavior of electrons in solids is governed by the Schrödinger equation, which describes the wave-like nature of particles. When an electron encounters a potential barrier, it can either be reflected or transmitted, depending on the energy of the electron and the height and width of the barrier.

The probability of an electron tunneling through a barrier decreases exponentially with increasing barrier width, similar to the behavior of a quantum particle. This can be seen as a solution to the time-dependent Schrödinger equation, where the wave function of the electron decays exponentially as it moves through the barrier.

#### Hydrodynamic quantum analogs

In 2009, researchers demonstrated a macroscopic analogy of quantum tunneling using fluid droplets on a vibrating surface. The droplets were able to pass through barriers, similar to quantum particles undergoing tunneling. This experiment provided a visual representation of the quantum phenomenon and further solidified our understanding of quantum tunneling in solids.

#### Quantized orbits

Another important concept that arises from the quantum nature of electrons in solids is the quantization of energy levels. When two atomic particles interact and form a bound state, such as in the case of a hydrogen atom, the energy spectrum is discrete. This means that the energy levels of the bound state are not continuous and only exist in discrete quantities, forming "quantized orbits."

On the macroscopic level, this can be seen in the interaction of two walking fluid droplets on a vibrating surface. The droplets can form bound states and exhibit quantized orbits, similar to the behavior of electrons in atoms.

In conclusion, quantum tunneling is a fundamental phenomenon in solids that arises from the wave-like nature of particles. It plays a crucial role in the behavior of electrons in materials and has been demonstrated in both microscopic and macroscopic systems. Understanding quantum tunneling is essential for understanding the electronic properties of materials and is a key concept in the field of solid state physics.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 15.2 Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 15.2b Tunneling in Superconductors

Superconductors are materials that exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a certain critical temperature. In monolayers of molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>), superconductivity has been observed at temperatures below 9.4 K when an electric field is applied. This phenomenon is still not fully understood, but it is believed to be related to the quantum tunneling of electrons in these materials.

#### 15.2b Tunneling in Superconductors

In superconductors, electrons form Cooper pairs due to the attractive interaction between them. These Cooper pairs are able to tunnel through potential barriers due to their wave-like nature, allowing for the flow of electrical current without any resistance. This is known as the Josephson effect, named after Brian David Josephson who first predicted it in 1962.

The Josephson effect has been observed in various superconducting materials, including MoS<sub>2</sub> monolayers. This effect is crucial for the functioning of superconducting devices such as SQUIDs (superconducting quantum interference devices) and Josephson junctions.

#### Symmetry-filtering in tunnel barriers

The behavior of electrons in tunnel barriers is not only affected by the height and width of the barrier, but also by the symmetry of the barrier. This was first demonstrated in the use of epitaxial magnesium oxide (MgO) as a tunnel barrier in MTJs (magnetic tunnel junctions). MgO has a specific symmetry that filters the tunneling transmission of electrons, resulting in a large increase in tunnel magnetoresistance (TMR).

In MTJs with parallel (P) magnetization of the electrodes, electrons with a particular symmetry dominate the junction current. However, in the antiparallel (AP) state, this channel is blocked and electrons with a different symmetry dominate the current. This results in a significant difference in TMR between the two states.

#### Hydrodynamic quantum analogs

In 2009, a macroscopic analogy of quantum tunneling was demonstrated using fluid droplets on a vibrating surface. This experiment provided a visual representation of the exponential decay of the wave function as the droplet passed through a barrier, similar to the behavior of quantum particles.

This concept has also been applied to the study of superconductors, where the behavior of Cooper pairs can be described using hydrodynamic equations. This has led to a better understanding of the Josephson effect and the role of quantum tunneling in superconductors.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section 15.2: Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

Quantum tunneling is a phenomenon in which particles are able to pass through potential barriers that would normally be impossible to overcome according to classical mechanics. This is a result of the wave-like nature of particles at the quantum level. In solids, quantum tunneling has important implications for various applications in electronics, superconductivity, and nanotechnology.

### Subsection 15.2c: Quantum Tunneling Devices

Quantum tunneling devices utilize the principles of quantum tunneling to perform various functions in electronics and nanotechnology. These devices take advantage of the unique properties of materials at the quantum level to achieve high performance and efficiency.

#### Cold Emission

One application of quantum tunneling in solids is in cold emission of electrons. This phenomenon is relevant to semiconductors and superconductors, and is similar to thermionic emission. In cold emission, electrons are able to tunnel out of the atomic state due to a strong electric field, resulting in a current that varies exponentially with the electric field. This is important for flash memory, vacuum tubes, and electron microscopes.

#### Tunnel Junctions

Tunnel junctions are created by separating two conductors with a very thin insulator. These junctions are crucial for understanding quantum tunneling and have various applications. One example is the Josephson junction, which takes advantage of quantum tunneling and superconductivity to create the Josephson effect. This effect has applications in precision measurements of voltages and magnetic fields, as well as in multijunction solar cells.

#### Quantum-Dot Cellular Automata

Quantum-dot cellular automata (QCA) is a molecular binary logic synthesis technology that operates by the inter-island electron tunneling system. This is a low power and fast device that can operate at a maximum frequency of 15 PHz. QCAs have potential applications in high-speed computing and communication systems.

#### Tunnel Diode

Tunnel diodes are electrical semiconductor devices that utilize quantum tunneling to allow for current flow in one direction. These diodes have various applications in electronics, including in oscillators, amplifiers, and high-frequency circuits.

In conclusion, quantum tunneling in solids has a wide range of applications in electronics, superconductivity, and nanotechnology. By understanding and harnessing the principles of quantum mechanics, we are able to create advanced devices with high performance and efficiency. 


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: 15.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

Quantum entanglement is a phenomenon in which two or more particles become connected in such a way that the state of one particle is dependent on the state of the other, even when they are separated by large distances. This concept was first introduced in the famous thought experiment known as the "EPR paradox" by Albert Einstein, Boris Podolsky, and Nathan Rosen in 1935. It was later experimentally confirmed by John Bell in 1964, and has since become an important topic in quantum mechanics.

### Subsection: 15.3a Quantum Entanglement and its Implications

Quantum entanglement has significant implications for the study of solids. In our system of interest, let us consider two particles, <math>S</math> and <math>O</math>, which are entangled with each other. This means that the state of <math>O</math> is dependent on the state of <math>S</math>, and vice versa. This entanglement can be described by a mathematical operator, <math>M</math>, which has an eigenvalue of 1 when the states of <math>S</math> and <math>O</math> are accurately reflected, and 0 when they are not.

One of the consequences of quantum entanglement is the phenomenon of coherence. This refers to the ability of a system to maintain its quantum state over time, even when it is not being observed. In our system, <math>O</math> can predict with certainty that the <math>S+O</math> system is in "some" eigenstate of <math>M</math> at time <math>t_2</math>, but cannot determine "which" eigenstate it is in without interacting with the system. This distinction between knowing "that" and knowing "what" is a common one in everyday life, and is also relevant in the study of solids.

An interesting implication of quantum entanglement is the apparent paradox that arises when comparing the outcomes of measurements between two observers. In the problem of the observer observed, if <math>O'</math> has the full Hamiltonians of both <math>S</math> and <math>O</math>, he can determine with certainty "that" at time <math>t_2</math>, <math>O</math> has a determinate result for <math>S</math>'s spin, but not "what" that result is without interaction. This highlights the importance of interactions in determining the state of a system, and the limitations of knowledge in quantum mechanics.

In solids, quantum entanglement has important implications for various applications. One example is in quantum computing, where entangled particles can be used to perform calculations and store information. It also has potential applications in quantum cryptography, where entangled particles can be used to securely transmit information. Additionally, the study of quantum entanglement in solids can provide insights into the fundamental nature of quantum mechanics and the behavior of particles at the nanoscale.

In conclusion, quantum entanglement is a fascinating phenomenon with significant implications for the study of solids. Its study has led to a deeper understanding of quantum mechanics and has potential applications in various fields. As we continue to explore the quantum world, the role of entanglement in solids will undoubtedly play a crucial role in shaping our understanding of the universe.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: 15.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

Quantum entanglement is a phenomenon in which two or more particles become connected in such a way that the state of one particle is dependent on the state of the other, even when they are separated by large distances. This concept was first introduced in the famous thought experiment known as the "EPR paradox" by Albert Einstein, Boris Podolsky, and Nathan Rosen in 1935. It was later experimentally confirmed by John Bell in 1964, and has since become an important topic in quantum mechanics.

### Subsection: 15.3a Quantum Entanglement and its Implications

Quantum entanglement has significant implications for the study of solids. In our system of interest, let us consider two particles, <math>S</math> and <math>O</math>, which are entangled with each other. This means that the state of <math>O</math> is dependent on the state of <math>S</math>, and vice versa. This entanglement can be described by a mathematical operator, <math>M</math>, which has an eigenvalue of 1 when the states of <math>S</math> and <math>O</math> are accurately reflected, and 0 when they are not.

One of the consequences of quantum entanglement is the phenomenon of coherence. This refers to the ability of a system to maintain its quantum state over time, even when it is not being observed. In our system, <math>O</math> can predict with certainty that the <math>S+O</math> system is in "some" eigenstate of <math>M</math> at time <math>t_2</math>, but cannot determine "which" eigenstate it is in without interacting with the system. This distinction between knowing "that" and knowing "what" is a common one in everyday life, and is also relevant in the study of solids.

An interesting implication of quantum entanglement is the apparent paradox that arises when comparing the outcomes of measurements between two observers. In the problem of the observer observed, if <math>S</math> and <math>O</math> are entangled, then the measurement of <math>S</math> will affect the state of <math>O</math>, even if they are separated by a large distance. This phenomenon, known as "spooky action at a distance," challenges our understanding of causality and locality in the quantum world.

### Subsection: 15.3b Entanglement in Solid State Systems

Quantum entanglement has been observed in various solid state systems, such as superconductors, quantum dots, and spin chains. In these systems, the entanglement arises from the interactions between the constituent particles, such as electrons or atoms. The entanglement can be controlled and manipulated by external factors, such as temperature, magnetic fields, and electric fields.

One example of entanglement in solid state systems is the phenomenon of superconductivity. In a superconductor, electrons form pairs known as Cooper pairs, which are entangled with each other. This entanglement allows for the flow of current without resistance, a property that is crucial for many technological applications.

Another example is the entanglement between electron spins in a quantum dot. By controlling the spin states of the electrons, researchers have been able to create entangled states that can be used for quantum information processing and communication.

The study of entanglement in solid state systems has also led to the development of new materials and technologies. For example, topological insulators, which are materials that have conducting surface states but insulating bulk states, have been found to exhibit strong entanglement between the surface electrons. This has potential applications in quantum computing and spintronics.

In conclusion, quantum entanglement plays a crucial role in the behavior of solids and has significant implications for our understanding of the quantum world. Its study has led to new discoveries and technologies, and continues to be a fascinating area of research in the field of solid state physics.

## Chapter 15: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: 15.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

Quantum entanglement is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that has significant implications for the study of solids. In this section, we will explore the phenomenon of quantum entanglement and its implications for the behavior of solids.

### Subsection: 15.3c Quantum Computing and Information

Quantum computing is a rapidly advancing field that utilizes the principles of quantum mechanics to perform calculations and process information. Unlike classical computers, which use binary bits to represent information, quantum computers use quantum bits, or qubits, which can exist in multiple states simultaneously. This allows for the potential of exponentially faster computing and the ability to solve complex problems that are currently intractable for classical computers.

One of the key components of quantum computing is quantum entanglement. By entangling qubits, quantum computers can perform calculations and process information in a way that is not possible with classical computers. This is because entangled qubits can share information instantaneously, regardless of the distance between them. This phenomenon is known as quantum teleportation and is a crucial aspect of quantum computing.

In recent years, there has been significant progress in the development of quantum computers. In 2019, Google announced that they had achieved quantum supremacy, meaning that their quantum computer had solved a problem that would take a classical computer thousands of years to solve, in just 200 seconds. This breakthrough has opened up new possibilities for quantum computing and has sparked a race among companies and researchers to develop more powerful and scalable quantum computers.

Aside from its potential for faster computing, quantum entanglement also has implications for quantum information. In classical information theory, information is encoded in bits, which can only have a value of 0 or 1. However, in quantum information theory, information can be encoded in qubits, which can exist in multiple states simultaneously. This allows for the potential of more efficient and secure communication and data storage.

In conclusion, quantum entanglement is a crucial concept in quantum mechanics that has significant implications for the study of solids. Its potential for faster computing and more efficient information processing has led to rapid advancements in the field of quantum computing. As we continue to explore the principles of quantum mechanics, it is clear that quantum entanglement will play a crucial role in shaping the future of technology and information processing.
