## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of quantum mechanics as it applies to solids. Quantum mechanics is a fundamental theory that describes the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level. It has revolutionized our understanding of the physical world and has had a profound impact on various fields, including solid state physics.

We will begin by exploring the basic principles of quantum mechanics, such as wave-particle duality and the uncertainty principle. We will then move on to discuss the quantum mechanical description of solids, including the concept of energy bands and the role of electrons in determining the properties of solids.

Next, we will delve into the theory of crystal structures and how they relate to the electronic properties of solids. We will also cover topics such as the Bloch theorem and Brillouin zones, which are essential in understanding the electronic structure of crystals.

Finally, we will discuss some of the key applications of quantum mechanics in solid state physics, such as the band theory of metals and semiconductors, and the phenomenon of superconductivity.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the study of solids. This knowledge will serve as a strong foundation for further exploration into the fascinating world of solid state physics. So let's dive in and discover the quantum mechanical secrets of solids!


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.1a Quantum Numbers and Quantum States

In the previous chapter, we discussed the basic principles of quantum mechanics and how they apply to the study of particles at the atomic and subatomic level. In this chapter, we will explore how these principles can be applied to the study of solids.

One of the key concepts in quantum mechanics is the idea of quantum states. These states describe the possible energy levels that a particle can occupy. In the case of solids, the particles we are interested in are electrons. Just like in the case of atoms, electrons in solids can only occupy certain energy levels, or quantum states.

To fully describe a quantum state, we need to specify its quantum numbers. These numbers, denoted by <math>n</math>, <math>\ell</math>, and <math>m</math>, are integers that determine the energy, angular momentum, and orientation of the electron in the solid. The values of these quantum numbers are restricted by certain rules, which we will discuss in more detail in this section.

The first quantum number, <math>n</math>, is known as the principal quantum number and determines the energy of the electron. It can take on values of 1, 2, 3, and so on, with each value corresponding to a different energy level. The higher the value of <math>n</math>, the higher the energy level.

The second quantum number, <math>\ell</math>, is known as the azimuthal quantum number and determines the angular momentum of the electron. It can take on values from 0 to <math>n-1</math>, with each value corresponding to a different subshell. For example, when <math>n=1</math>, <math>\ell</math> can only be 0, meaning there is only one possible subshell. But when <math>n=2</math>, <math>\ell</math> can be either 0 or 1, meaning there are two possible subshells.

The third quantum number, <math>m</math>, is known as the magnetic quantum number and determines the orientation of the electron's angular momentum. It can take on values from <math>-l</math> to <math>l</math>, with each value corresponding to a different orbital within the subshell. For example, when <math>\ell=1</math>, <math>m</math> can be -1, 0, or 1, meaning there are three possible orbitals within that subshell.

It is important to note that these quantum numbers are not arbitrary, but are determined by the symmetries of the solid. This is where the group-theoretical interpretation mentioned in the context becomes relevant. By understanding the symmetries of the solid, we can determine the allowed values of <math>n</math>, <math>\ell</math>, and <math>m</math> and gain a deeper understanding of the quantum states in solids.

In addition to these quantum numbers, we also need to consider the electron's spin, which is described by the spin quantum number <math>s</math>. The spin can take on two values, either +1/2 or -1/2, and is responsible for the magnetic properties of the electron.

Just like in the case of atoms, the quantum numbers of electrons in solids are quantized, meaning they can only take on certain discrete values. This is a fundamental principle of quantum mechanics and has important implications for the behavior of electrons in solids.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of angular momentum in more detail and see how it relates to the quantum numbers and states of electrons in solids. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.1b Pauli Exclusion Principle

In the previous section, we discussed the basic principles of quantum states in solids, including the three quantum numbers that describe the energy, angular momentum, and orientation of electrons in a solid. In this section, we will explore the Pauli exclusion principle, which plays a crucial role in determining the behavior of electrons in solids.

The Pauli exclusion principle states that two or more identical fermions (particles with half-integer spin) cannot occupy the same quantum state within a quantum system simultaneously. This principle was first formulated by Austrian physicist Wolfgang Pauli in 1925 for electrons, and later extended to all fermions with his spin-statistics theorem of 1940.

In the case of electrons in atoms, the Pauli exclusion principle can be stated as follows: it is impossible for two electrons of a poly-electron atom to have the same values of the four quantum numbers: "n", the principal quantum number; "<ell>", the azimuthal quantum number; "m<sub><ell></sub>", the magnetic quantum number; and "m<sub>s</sub>", the spin quantum number. This means that if two electrons reside in the same orbital, then their "n", "<ell>", and "m<sub><ell></sub>" values are the same; therefore their "m<sub>s</sub>" must be different, and thus the electrons must have opposite half-integer spin projections of 1/2 and −1/2.

This principle has important implications for the electronic structure of solids. In a solid, electrons are confined to a lattice structure and are subject to the potential energy of the surrounding atoms. As a result, the energy levels available to electrons in a solid are different from those in an isolated atom. However, the Pauli exclusion principle still applies, meaning that each energy level can only accommodate a certain number of electrons with different spin states.

For example, in a simple one-dimensional lattice, the energy levels are quantized and can be represented by a series of energy bands. Each band can accommodate a certain number of electrons, with the lower energy bands being filled first. This is known as the Aufbau principle, which states that electrons will fill the lowest energy levels available before moving to higher energy levels.

The Pauli exclusion principle also plays a crucial role in determining the properties of materials. For instance, it is responsible for the stability of matter and the formation of chemical bonds. Without this principle, electrons would be able to occupy the same energy level, resulting in unstable and unpredictable behavior.

In summary, the Pauli exclusion principle is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that governs the behavior of electrons in solids. It restricts the number of electrons that can occupy a given energy level and plays a crucial role in determining the electronic structure and properties of materials. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.1 Quantum States in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.1c Fermi Energy and Fermi Surface

In the previous section, we discussed the basic principles of quantum states in solids, including the three quantum numbers that describe the energy, angular momentum, and orientation of electrons in a solid. In this section, we will explore the concept of Fermi energy and Fermi surface, which are crucial in understanding the electronic properties of solids.

The Fermi energy, named after Italian physicist Enrico Fermi, is the highest energy level occupied by electrons at absolute zero temperature. It is a fundamental property of a solid and is determined by the number of electrons and the energy levels available to them. The Fermi energy is often represented by the symbol $E_F$ and is typically measured in electron volts (eV).

At absolute zero temperature, all electrons in a solid will occupy the lowest available energy levels, filling up the energy levels from the bottom up according to the Pauli exclusion principle. The Fermi energy is then the energy level at which the last electron is added. This energy level is also known as the Fermi level and is an important reference point for understanding the electronic properties of a solid.

The Fermi surface, on the other hand, is a representation of the boundary between the occupied and unoccupied energy levels at absolute zero temperature. It is a three-dimensional surface in the momentum space, where the momentum of an electron is plotted against its energy. The shape of the Fermi surface is determined by the crystal structure of the solid and the number of electrons in the system.

The Fermi surface plays a crucial role in determining the electrical and thermal conductivity of a solid. In metals, where the Fermi energy lies within the conduction band, the Fermi surface is a continuous surface. This allows for the easy movement of electrons, resulting in high electrical and thermal conductivity. In insulators, where the Fermi energy lies within the band gap, the Fermi surface is a discontinuous surface, making it difficult for electrons to move and resulting in low conductivity.

In semiconductors, the Fermi energy lies close to the middle of the band gap, resulting in a small Fermi surface. This allows for some movement of electrons, leading to intermediate levels of conductivity. The Fermi surface also plays a crucial role in determining the optical and magnetic properties of a solid.

In summary, the Fermi energy and Fermi surface are important concepts in understanding the electronic properties of solids. They provide a fundamental understanding of the behavior of electrons in a solid and play a crucial role in determining its physical properties. In the next section, we will explore the concept of band theory, which builds upon the concept of Fermi energy and Fermi surface to explain the electronic structure of solids.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.2 Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.2a Quantum Tunneling Phenomenon

Quantum tunneling is a fundamental phenomenon in quantum mechanics that plays a crucial role in the behavior of particles in solids. In classical mechanics, a particle must have enough energy to overcome a potential barrier in order to pass through it. However, in the quantum realm, particles can exhibit wave-like behavior and have a finite probability of passing through a potential barrier, even if they do not have enough energy to do so classically. This phenomenon is known as quantum tunneling.

The probability of quantum tunneling decreases exponentially with increasing barrier width, as shown by the solution to the time-dependent Schrödinger equation. This behavior is analogous to the behavior of walking droplets in a vibrating fluid bath, where the probability of the droplet passing through a barrier also decreases exponentially with increasing barrier width. This macroscopic analogy, first demonstrated in 2009, provides a visual representation of the quantum tunneling phenomenon and helps to deepen our understanding of it.

In addition to its role in understanding the behavior of particles in solids, quantum tunneling also has practical applications. For example, it is essential in the operation of tunneling diodes, which are used in electronic devices such as transistors and computer memory. It also plays a crucial role in the process of nuclear fusion, where particles must tunnel through a potential barrier in order to overcome the repulsive forces between them.

Overall, quantum tunneling is a fascinating and important phenomenon in the study of solids. Its understanding is crucial in explaining the behavior of particles in solids and has practical applications in various fields. In the next section, we will explore another important concept in the quantum mechanics of solids: quantized orbits.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.2 Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.2b Tunneling in Superconductors

Superconductivity is a phenomenon in which certain materials exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a critical temperature. In monolayers of molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>), superconductivity has been observed at temperatures below 9.4 K when an electric field is applied. This discovery has opened up new possibilities for studying superconductivity in two-dimensional materials.

One of the key concepts in understanding superconductivity is the phenomenon of quantum tunneling. In classical mechanics, a particle must have enough energy to overcome a potential barrier in order to pass through it. However, in the quantum realm, particles can exhibit wave-like behavior and have a finite probability of passing through a potential barrier, even if they do not have enough energy to do so classically. This phenomenon is known as quantum tunneling.

In the context of superconductors, quantum tunneling plays a crucial role in the behavior of Cooper pairs, which are pairs of electrons that are bound together at low temperatures. These Cooper pairs are able to tunnel through the potential barrier created by the lattice of the superconductor, allowing for the flow of electrical current without any resistance.

The probability of quantum tunneling decreases exponentially with increasing barrier width, as shown by the solution to the time-dependent Schrödinger equation. This behavior is analogous to the behavior of walking droplets in a vibrating fluid bath, where the probability of the droplet passing through a barrier also decreases exponentially with increasing barrier width. This macroscopic analogy, first demonstrated in 2009, provides a visual representation of the quantum tunneling phenomenon and helps to deepen our understanding of it.

In addition to its role in understanding superconductivity, quantum tunneling also has practical applications in the field of spintronics. Spintronics is a branch of electronics that utilizes the spin of electrons, in addition to their charge, to store and process information. In particular, the phenomenon of tunnel magnetoresistance (TMR) has been extensively studied in the context of spintronics. TMR is the change in electrical resistance of a material when the magnetization of two electrodes is either parallel or antiparallel. This effect is greatly enhanced in superconductors, where the Cooper pairs are able to tunnel through the potential barrier with a particular symmetry that is fully spin-polarized.

The introduction of epitaxial magnesium oxide (MgO) as a tunnel barrier in magnetic tunnel junctions (MTJs) has greatly increased the TMR values, reflecting a synergetic combination of electrode and barrier electronic structures. This has allowed for the engineering of the junction's potential landscape for electrons of a given symmetry, providing further evidence for the impact of the barrier's electronic structure on tunnelling spintronics.

In conclusion, quantum tunneling is a fundamental phenomenon in quantum mechanics that plays a crucial role in the behavior of particles in solids. Its understanding is essential in explaining the behavior of Cooper pairs in superconductors and has practical applications in the field of spintronics. In the next section, we will explore another important aspect of quantum tunneling in solids - its role in the phenomenon of tunneling magnetoresistance.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.2 Quantum Tunneling in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.2c Quantum Tunneling Devices

Quantum tunneling is a fundamental phenomenon in quantum mechanics that plays a crucial role in the behavior of solids. In this section, we will explore the use of quantum tunneling in the development of quantum tunneling devices, which have the potential to revolutionize the field of electronics.

One of the key applications of quantum tunneling in solids is in the development of quantum dot cellular automata (QCA). QCA is a promising technology that utilizes the quantum properties of electrons to perform logic operations. It is based on the concept of quantum dots, which are small regions of a material that confine electrons in three dimensions. These quantum dots act as the basic building blocks of QCA devices, and their behavior is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics.

There are four different classes of QCA implementations: metal-island, semiconductor, molecular, and magnetic. The metal-island implementation was the first fabrication technology created to demonstrate the concept of QCA. It involves building quantum dots using aluminum islands, which are kept at extremely low temperatures to observe quantum effects. While this method was not intended to compete with current technology, it paved the way for further advancements in QCA.

The semiconductor implementation of QCA has the potential to utilize highly advanced semiconductor fabrication processes, similar to those used in CMOS devices. In this method, cell polarization is encoded as charge position, and quantum-dot interactions rely on electrostatic coupling. However, current semiconductor processes have not yet reached a point where mass production of devices with small features (≈20 nanometers) is possible. This limitation has led to the use of serial lithography, which is slow, expensive, and unsuitable for mass production. Despite these challenges, most QCA prototyping experiments are currently done using this implementation technology.

Another proposed method for implementing QCA devices is through the use of single molecules. This approach has the potential to offer highly symmetric QCA cell structures, extremely high switching speeds, and operation at room temperature. Additionally, the self-assembly of molecules could potentially enable mass production of devices. However, this method has not yet been implemented and remains a topic of ongoing research.

In conclusion, quantum tunneling devices have the potential to revolutionize the field of electronics by utilizing the principles of quantum mechanics. While there are still challenges to overcome in terms of fabrication and scalability, the development of QCA and other quantum tunneling devices holds great promise for the future of technology.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.3a Quantum Entanglement and its Implications

Quantum entanglement is a phenomenon in quantum mechanics where two or more particles become connected in such a way that the state of one particle is dependent on the state of the other, even when they are separated by large distances. This concept was first introduced by Albert Einstein, Boris Podolsky, and Nathan Rosen in their famous EPR paper in 1935. However, it was not until the 1960s that John Bell's theorem proved the existence of entanglement and its implications for quantum mechanics.

In solids, quantum entanglement plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of particles and their interactions. The concept of entanglement is closely related to the idea of coherence, which is the ability of a system to maintain a stable and consistent state. In our system above, the observer <math>O'</math> may be interested in ascertaining whether or not the state of <math>O</math> accurately reflects the state of <math>S</math>. This can be represented by an operator <math>M</math>, with an eigenvalue of 1 indicating that <math>O</math> accurately reflects the state of <math>S</math>. However, due to the entanglement between <math>O</math> and <math>S</math>, the observer <math>O'</math> cannot determine the exact state of <math>O</math> without interacting with the system.

This has important implications for the measurement of quantum systems. At time <math>t_2</math>, <math>O'</math> can predict with certainty that the <math>S+O</math> system is in "some" eigenstate of <math>M</math>, but cannot determine "which" eigenstate it is in without interaction. This distinction between knowing "that" and knowing "what" is a common one in everyday life, and it highlights the limitations of our understanding of quantum systems.

An interesting paradox arises when comparing the results of measurements between two observers. In the problem of the observer observed section above, if <math>O'</math> has the full Hamiltonians of both <math>S</math> and <math>O</math>, he can determine with certainty "that" at time <math>t_2</math>, <math>O</math> has a determinate result for <math>S</math>'s spin. However, he cannot determine "what" <math>O</math>'s result is without interaction, as he does not know his own Hamiltonian. This highlights the importance of interactions in quantum systems and the limitations of our knowledge.

The concept of quantum entanglement has also led to the development of quantum entanglement-based technologies, such as quantum cryptography and quantum teleportation. These technologies utilize the entanglement of particles to securely transmit information and teleport quantum states between distant locations. This has the potential to revolutionize the field of communication and computing, and further research in this area is ongoing.

In conclusion, quantum entanglement is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of solids. Its implications for measurement and the development of new technologies make it a fascinating and important topic in the study of solids. 


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.3b Entanglement in Solid State Systems

Quantum entanglement, as discussed in the previous section, is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that has important implications for the behavior of particles and their interactions. In this section, we will explore how entanglement manifests in solid state systems and its potential applications.

One of the key features of solid state systems is the presence of many particles, often in a highly correlated state. This makes them ideal for studying entanglement, as the interactions between particles can lead to the creation of entangled states. In fact, solid state systems have been used to demonstrate some of the most striking examples of entanglement, such as the famous Bell test experiments.

One way to understand entanglement in solid state systems is through the concept of spin. In quantum mechanics, particles such as electrons have an intrinsic property called spin, which can be either "up" or "down". When two particles are entangled, their spins become correlated, meaning that if one particle is measured to have an "up" spin, the other particle will also have an "up" spin, regardless of the distance between them.

This phenomenon has been observed in various solid state systems, such as quantum dots and superconducting circuits. In these systems, the entanglement between particles can be controlled and manipulated, allowing for the creation of complex entangled states. This has potential applications in quantum information processing, where entanglement is a crucial resource for tasks such as quantum teleportation and quantum cryptography.

Another interesting aspect of entanglement in solid state systems is its connection to topological order. Topological order refers to the organization of particles in a material that is not affected by small changes in the system. This can lead to the emergence of exotic states of matter, such as topological insulators, which have potential applications in quantum computing.

In conclusion, entanglement in solid state systems is a rich and fascinating area of research that has the potential to revolutionize our understanding of quantum mechanics and lead to new technologies. As we continue to explore the quantum world of solids, it is clear that entanglement will play a crucial role in shaping our understanding of these complex systems.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 18: Quantum Mechanics of Solids:

### Section: - Section: 18.3 Quantum Entanglement in Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 18.3c Quantum Computing and Information

Quantum entanglement, as discussed in the previous section, is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that has important implications for the behavior of particles and their interactions. In this section, we will explore how entanglement manifests in solid state systems and its potential applications in quantum computing and information.

One of the key features of solid state systems is the presence of many particles, often in a highly correlated state. This makes them ideal for studying entanglement, as the interactions between particles can lead to the creation of entangled states. In fact, solid state systems have been used to demonstrate some of the most striking examples of entanglement, such as the famous Bell test experiments.

One way to understand entanglement in solid state systems is through the concept of spin. In quantum mechanics, particles such as electrons have an intrinsic property called spin, which can be either "up" or "down". When two particles are entangled, their spins become correlated, meaning that if one particle is measured to have an "up" spin, the other particle will also have an "up" spin, regardless of the distance between them.

This phenomenon has been observed in various solid state systems, such as quantum dots and superconducting circuits. In these systems, the entanglement between particles can be controlled and manipulated, allowing for the creation of complex entangled states. This has potential applications in quantum information processing, where entanglement is a crucial resource for tasks such as quantum teleportation and quantum cryptography.

Another interesting aspect of entanglement in solid state systems is its connection to topological order. Topological order refers to the organization of particles in a material that is not dependent on the specific arrangement of the particles. This means that even if the particles are moved or rearranged, the overall topological properties of the material remain the same. This is similar to how a knot can be untied and retied, but it is still considered the same knot.

Topological order is closely related to entanglement, as it is a result of the entanglement between particles in the material. This has led to the development of topological quantum computing, where topologically ordered materials are used to store and manipulate quantum information. This approach has the potential to overcome some of the challenges faced by traditional quantum computing, such as decoherence and error correction.

In addition to quantum computing, entanglement in solid state systems has also been explored for applications in quantum information. This includes quantum communication, where entangled particles can be used to transmit information securely over long distances. It also has potential applications in quantum sensing, where entangled particles can be used to measure and detect small changes in a system.

Overall, the study of quantum entanglement in solid state systems has opened up new possibilities for quantum computing and information. With ongoing research and advancements in technology, we can expect to see even more exciting developments in this field in the future.

Furthermore, we have seen how the band theory can be extended to explain the properties of semiconductors and insulators. Finally, we have discussed the concept of phonons and how they contribute to the thermal and mechanical properties of solids.

The study of quantum mechanics of solids is a vast and complex field, and this chapter has only scratched the surface. However, it has provided a solid foundation for further exploration and understanding of this fascinating subject. By understanding the behavior of electrons and phonons in solids, we can gain a deeper understanding of the properties of materials and how they can be manipulated for various applications.

## Chapter: - Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of crystallography and diffraction in the context of solid state physics. Crystallography is the study of the arrangement of atoms in a crystal lattice, while diffraction is the phenomenon of light or other waves being scattered by a regular array of points or obstacles. These two topics are closely related and play a crucial role in understanding the properties and behavior of solids.

We will begin by discussing the basic principles of crystallography, including the different types of crystal lattices and the methods used to describe and classify them. We will also explore the concept of symmetry in crystals and its significance in determining their physical properties.

Next, we will delve into the topic of diffraction, which is a powerful tool for studying the structure of crystals. We will cover the fundamentals of diffraction, including the Bragg equation and the diffraction patterns produced by different types of crystals. We will also discuss the various techniques used to analyze diffraction patterns and extract information about the crystal structure.

Finally, we will explore the applications of crystallography and diffraction in solid state physics. This includes the use of X-ray diffraction to determine the crystal structure of materials, as well as the role of crystallography in understanding the electronic and magnetic properties of solids.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of crystallography and diffraction and their importance in the study of solids. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for further exploration of the fascinating world of solid state physics. So let's dive in and discover the wonders of crystallography and diffraction!


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section 19.1: Crystal Structures and Symmetry:

Crystal structures are the fundamental building blocks of solid materials. They are described in terms of the geometry of arrangement of particles in the unit cells. The unit cell is defined as the smallest repeating unit having the full symmetry of the crystal structure. The geometry of the unit cell is defined as a parallelepiped, providing six lattice parameters taken as the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). The positions of particles inside the unit cell are described by the fractional coordinates ($x_i$, $y_i$, $z_i$) along the cell edges, measured from a reference point. It is thus only necessary to report the coordinates of a smallest asymmetric subset of particles, called the crystallographic asymmetric unit. The asymmetric unit may be chosen so that it occupies the smallest physical space, which means that not all particles need to be physically located inside the boundaries given by the lattice parameters. All other particles of the unit cell are generated by the symmetry operations that characterize the symmetry of the unit cell. The collection of symmetry operations of the unit cell is expressed formally as the space group of the crystal structure.

### Subsection 19.1a: Crystal Structures and Unit Cells

The unit cell is the basic building block of a crystal structure. It is the smallest repeating unit that has the full symmetry of the crystal. The geometry of the unit cell is defined by six lattice parameters, which are the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). These parameters can be used to describe the shape and size of the unit cell.

There are seven basic types of crystal lattices, known as the seven crystal systems. These include cubic, tetragonal, orthorhombic, monoclinic, triclinic, hexagonal, and rhombohedral. Each of these systems has a unique set of lattice parameters and symmetry operations that define the unit cell.

The positions of particles within the unit cell are described by fractional coordinates along the cell edges. These coordinates are measured from a reference point and can be used to determine the positions of all particles within the unit cell. The smallest asymmetric subset of particles, known as the crystallographic asymmetric unit, is used to describe the crystal structure. This subset is chosen so that it occupies the smallest physical space, and all other particles in the unit cell can be generated by symmetry operations.

The symmetry of a crystal structure plays a crucial role in determining its physical properties. Symmetry operations, such as rotation, reflection, and translation, can be used to describe the repeating patterns within a crystal. The collection of all symmetry operations of a crystal is known as its space group. This group is used to classify and describe the symmetry of a crystal structure.

In summary, crystal structures are described in terms of the geometry of arrangement of particles in the unit cell. The unit cell is the smallest repeating unit that has the full symmetry of the crystal, and its geometry is defined by six lattice parameters. The positions of particles within the unit cell are described by fractional coordinates, and the symmetry of the crystal is characterized by its space group. Understanding crystal structures and their symmetry is essential for studying the properties and behavior of solids.

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section 19.1: Crystal Structures and Symmetry:

Crystal structures are the fundamental building blocks of solid materials. They are described in terms of the geometry of arrangement of particles in the unit cells. The unit cell is defined as the smallest repeating unit having the full symmetry of the crystal structure. The geometry of the unit cell is defined as a parallelepiped, providing six lattice parameters taken as the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). The positions of particles inside the unit cell are described by the fractional coordinates ($x_i$, $y_i$, $z_i$) along the cell edges, measured from a reference point. It is thus only necessary to report the coordinates of a smallest asymmetric subset of particles, called the crystallographic asymmetric unit. The asymmetric unit may be chosen so that it occupies the smallest physical space, which means that not all particles need to be physically located inside the boundaries given by the lattice parameters. All other particles of the unit cell are generated by the symmetry operations that characterize the symmetry of the unit cell. The collection of symmetry operations of the unit cell is expressed formally as the space group of the crystal structure.

### Subsection 19.1a: Crystal Structures and Unit Cells

The unit cell is the basic building block of a crystal structure. It is the smallest repeating unit that has the full symmetry of the crystal. The geometry of the unit cell is defined by six lattice parameters, which are the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). These parameters can be used to describe the shape and size of the unit cell.

There are seven basic types of crystal lattices, known as the seven crystal systems. These include cubic, tetragonal, orthorhombic, monoclinic, triclinic, hexagonal, and rhombohedral. Each crystal system has its own unique set of lattice parameters and symmetry operations, which determine the overall structure of the crystal.

The unit cell can be further classified into primitive and non-primitive cells. A primitive cell contains only one lattice point, while a non-primitive cell contains more than one lattice point. The choice of which type of cell to use depends on the specific crystal structure and the desired level of detail in the description.

In addition to the lattice parameters, the unit cell also has a set of basis vectors that describe the arrangement of particles within the cell. These basis vectors, along with the lattice parameters, determine the overall symmetry of the crystal structure. The symmetry operations of the unit cell can be described using the concept of point groups, which are groups of symmetry operations that leave at least one point in the crystal structure unchanged.

The collection of all possible symmetry operations of a crystal structure is known as the space group. There are a total of 230 space groups, each with its own unique combination of symmetry operations. These space groups can be used to classify and describe the symmetry of any crystal structure.

In summary, the unit cell, lattice parameters, basis vectors, and symmetry operations are all essential components in describing the crystal structure of a solid material. Understanding these concepts is crucial in the study of solid state physics and materials science. In the following subsection, we will explore the relationship between symmetry operations and space groups in more detail.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section 19.1: Crystal Structures and Symmetry:

Crystal structures are the fundamental building blocks of solid materials. They are described in terms of the geometry of arrangement of particles in the unit cells. The unit cell is defined as the smallest repeating unit having the full symmetry of the crystal structure. The geometry of the unit cell is defined as a parallelepiped, providing six lattice parameters taken as the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). The positions of particles inside the unit cell are described by the fractional coordinates ($x_i$, $y_i$, $z_i$) along the cell edges, measured from a reference point. It is thus only necessary to report the coordinates of a smallest asymmetric subset of particles, called the crystallographic asymmetric unit. The asymmetric unit may be chosen so that it occupies the smallest physical space, which means that not all particles need to be physically located inside the boundaries given by the lattice parameters. All other particles of the unit cell are generated by the symmetry operations that characterize the symmetry of the unit cell. The collection of symmetry operations of the unit cell is expressed formally as the space group of the crystal structure.

### Subsection 19.1a: Crystal Structures and Unit Cells

The unit cell is the basic building block of a crystal structure. It is the smallest repeating unit that has the full symmetry of the crystal. The geometry of the unit cell is defined by six lattice parameters, which are the lengths of the cell edges ($a$, $b$, $c$) and the angles between them ($\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$). These parameters can be used to describe the shape and size of the unit cell.

There are seven basic types of crystal lattices, known as the seven crystal systems. These include cubic, tetragonal, orthorhombic, monoclinic, triclinic, hexagonal, and rhombohedral. Each crystal system has a unique combination of lattice parameters and symmetry operations, resulting in a distinct crystal structure.

The simplest and most symmetric crystal system is the cubic system, which has three equal lattice parameters ($a=b=c$) and all angles equal to 90 degrees ($\alpha=\beta=\gamma=90^\circ$). This system has four different types of centering: primitive, body-centered, face-centered, and base-centered. The primitive cubic lattice has particles only at the corners of the unit cell, while the other three types of centering have additional particles at the center of the cell or on the faces of the cell.

The tetragonal system also has three equal lattice parameters, but one angle is different from 90 degrees ($\alpha=\beta=\gamma\neq90^\circ$). This system has two types of centering: primitive and body-centered. The orthorhombic system has three unequal lattice parameters and all angles equal to 90 degrees ($\alpha=\beta=\gamma=90^\circ$). It has four types of centering: primitive, body-centered, face-centered, and base-centered.

The monoclinic system has three unequal lattice parameters and two angles equal to 90 degrees ($\alpha=\gamma=90^\circ$). It has two types of centering: primitive and base-centered. The triclinic system has three unequal lattice parameters and all angles different from 90 degrees ($\alpha\neq\beta\neq\gamma\neq90^\circ$). It has only primitive centering.

The hexagonal system has two equal lattice parameters and all angles equal to 90 degrees ($\alpha=\beta=90^\circ$, $\gamma=120^\circ$). It has one type of centering: primitive. The rhombohedral system has three equal lattice parameters and all angles equal to 90 degrees ($\alpha=\beta=\gamma\neq90^\circ$). It has one type of centering: primitive.

The distribution of the 14 Bravais lattices into the 7 crystal systems is summarized in the following table:

| Crystal System | Lattice Parameters | Centering |
|----------------|-------------------|-----------|
| Cubic          | $a=b=c$           | Primitive, Body-centered, Face-centered, Base-centered |
| Tetragonal     | $a=b\neq c$       | Primitive, Body-centered |
| Orthorhombic   | $a\neq b\neq c$   | Primitive, Body-centered, Face-centered, Base-centered |
| Monoclinic     | $a\neq b\neq c$   | Primitive, Base-centered |
| Triclinic      | $a\neq b\neq c$   | Primitive |
| Hexagonal      | $a=b\neq c$       | Primitive |
| Rhombohedral   | $a=b=c$           | Primitive |

The Bravais lattices were first studied by Moritz Ludwig Frankenheim in 1842, who initially found 15 lattices. However, this was later corrected to 14 by A. Bravais in 1848. These 14 lattices represent the maximum symmetry a structure with the given translational symmetry can have. All crystalline materials, with the exception of quasicrystals, must fit into one of these 14 Bravais lattices.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.2 X-ray Diffraction:

### Subsection: 19.2a Basics of X-ray Diffraction

X-ray diffraction is a powerful technique used to study the structure of crystalline materials. It is based on the principle of Bragg diffraction, which occurs when an incident X-ray beam hits a crystalline sample and is reflected by the atoms on certain lattice planes. This results in a diffracted beam that propagates in a different direction from the incident beam, with a scattering angle of 2θB.

The cross section of the diffracted beam may or may not be identical to that of the incident beam. In the case of strongly asymmetric reflections, the beam size in the diffraction plane can be significantly expanded or compressed. This is due to the fact that the exit angle of the diffracted beam is determined by the lattice spacing of the sample, while the incidence angle is determined by the size of the incident beam. This results in a projection effect, where the apparent lateral size of sample features parallel to the exit surface is downscaled in the image.

X-ray diffraction can be performed in either reflection geometry (Bragg case) or transmission geometry (Laue case). In the Bragg case, the incident and diffracted beams enter and leave through the same surface, while in the Laue case, the incident beam enters through one surface and the diffracted beam exits through another surface. Both geometries have their own advantages and are used for different types of experiments.

The intensity distribution in a topograph, or X-ray diffraction image, is determined by the symmetry of the crystal structure. A homogeneous sample with a regular crystal lattice will yield a flat image, while intensity modulations can be observed in samples with defects or imperfections. These modulations can provide valuable information about the structure and properties of the material.

In order to fully understand the diffraction pattern, it is important to have a good understanding of crystal structures and symmetry. The unit cell is the smallest repeating unit of a crystal structure, and its geometry is defined by six lattice parameters. These parameters can be used to describe the shape and size of the unit cell, and they are essential for interpreting X-ray diffraction data.

There are seven basic types of crystal lattices, known as the seven crystal systems. These include cubic, tetragonal, orthorhombic, monoclinic, triclinic, hexagonal, and rhombohedral. Each system has its own unique symmetry elements, which can be described using the space group notation.

In summary, X-ray diffraction is a powerful tool for studying the structure of crystalline materials. It is based on the principle of Bragg diffraction and can provide valuable information about the symmetry and defects in crystal structures. In order to fully interpret diffraction data, a good understanding of crystal structures and symmetry is essential. 


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.2 X-ray Diffraction:

### Subsection: 19.2b Bragg's Law and Crystal Structure Determination

In the previous section, we discussed the basics of X-ray diffraction and how it can be used to study the structure of crystalline materials. In this section, we will delve deeper into the theory behind X-ray diffraction and how it can be used to determine the crystal structure of a material.

The phenomenon of X-ray diffraction was first observed by Max von Laue in 1912, who discovered that X-rays could be diffracted by crystals. This was further studied by William Henry Bragg and his son William Lawrence Bragg, who developed the mathematical theory behind X-ray diffraction and proposed what is now known as Bragg's Law.

Bragg's Law states that for a crystal with a regular lattice structure, the X-rays will be diffracted at specific angles determined by the spacing between the crystal planes and the wavelength of the incident X-rays. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
n\lambda = 2d\sin\theta
$$

where $n$ is the order of the diffraction, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the X-rays, $d$ is the spacing between the crystal planes, and $\theta$ is the angle of diffraction.

This law allows us to determine the spacing between the crystal planes, which is directly related to the crystal structure of the material. By measuring the diffraction angles and using Bragg's Law, we can create a diffraction pattern that can be used to determine the crystal structure of the material.

In order to fully understand the diffraction pattern, we must also consider the symmetry of the crystal structure. The symmetry of the crystal will determine the intensity distribution of the diffraction pattern. For example, a crystal with a regular lattice will produce a simple diffraction pattern, while a crystal with defects or imperfections will produce a more complex pattern.

In addition to determining the crystal structure, X-ray diffraction can also provide information about the properties of the material. By analyzing the intensity and shape of the diffraction peaks, we can determine the crystal size, strain, and orientation of the material.

In conclusion, X-ray diffraction is a powerful tool for studying the structure of crystalline materials. By understanding the theory behind Bragg's Law and analyzing the diffraction pattern, we can determine the crystal structure and properties of a material. This technique has revolutionized the field of materials science and continues to be an essential tool for researchers in this field.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.2 X-ray Diffraction:

### Subsection: 19.2c Powder Diffraction and Single Crystal Diffraction

In the previous section, we discussed how X-ray diffraction can be used to determine the crystal structure of a material. However, the previous section focused on single crystal diffraction, where the crystal is oriented in a specific direction. In this section, we will explore how powder diffraction and single crystal diffraction differ and how they can both be used to study the structure of crystalline materials.

Powder diffraction is a technique used to study the structure of polycrystalline materials or crystal powders. Unlike single crystal diffraction, where the crystal is oriented in a specific direction, powder diffraction involves randomly oriented crystals. This results in a diffraction pattern that is more complex and difficult to interpret.

The diffraction pattern obtained from powder diffraction is a one-dimensional representation of the three-dimensional reciprocal space. This is due to the fact that the crystals are randomly oriented, resulting in a partial-to-total overlap of symmetry-independent reflections. This makes the structure determination process more difficult, if not impossible.

Despite the challenges posed by powder diffraction, it is still a valuable technique for studying the structure of crystalline materials. In fact, it is often used in conjunction with single crystal diffraction to obtain a more complete understanding of the crystal structure.

On the other hand, single crystal diffraction involves studying a single crystal in a specific orientation. This results in a simpler diffraction pattern, making it easier to interpret and analyze. However, obtaining a single crystal of sufficient size and quality can be challenging, making single crystal diffraction a more specialized technique.

Both powder diffraction and single crystal diffraction rely on Bragg's Law to determine the crystal structure of a material. By measuring the diffraction angles and using Bragg's Law, a diffraction pattern can be created that can be used to determine the spacing between crystal planes and the crystal structure of the material.

In addition to Bragg's Law, the symmetry of the crystal structure also plays a crucial role in the diffraction pattern. The symmetry of the crystal will determine the intensity distribution of the diffraction pattern. A crystal with a regular lattice will produce a simple diffraction pattern, while a crystal with defects or imperfections will produce a more complex pattern.

In conclusion, both powder diffraction and single crystal diffraction are important techniques for studying the structure of crystalline materials. While powder diffraction may be more challenging due to the random orientation of crystals, it is still a valuable tool in conjunction with single crystal diffraction. By understanding the principles behind these techniques, we can gain a deeper understanding of the structure of materials at the atomic level.


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.3 Electron and Neutron Diffraction:

### Subsection (optional): 19.3a Electron Diffraction Techniques

In the previous section, we discussed how X-ray diffraction can be used to determine the crystal structure of a material. However, X-ray diffraction is not the only technique available for studying crystal structures. In this section, we will explore how electrons and neutrons can also be used for diffraction experiments.

Electron diffraction in a transmission electron microscope (TEM) is a powerful technique for studying the structure of crystalline materials. In a TEM, a controlled beam of electrons is directed at a thin film of the material. The electrons interact with the atoms in the material, causing them to diffract. By analyzing the resulting diffraction pattern, information such as lattice constants, symmetries, and even unknown crystal structures can be determined.

One of the advantages of electron diffraction is its ability to provide localized information. Unlike X-ray diffraction, which provides an average structure of the entire crystal, electron diffraction can provide information from just a few or even single atoms. This makes it a valuable tool for studying nanomaterials and defects in crystals.

Electron diffraction can also be combined with other techniques, such as selected area diffraction, high-resolution imaging, and chemical analysis through energy-dispersive x-ray spectroscopy. This allows for a more comprehensive understanding of the crystal structure and properties.

In a TEM, the electron beam is manipulated by electron optics, including magnetic lenses, deflectors, and apertures. These elements control the incident beam, which can range from a wide and parallel beam to a converging cone smaller than an atom. As the beam interacts with the sample, part of it is diffracted and part is transmitted without changing direction. This diffraction pattern is then detected and analyzed.

Neutron diffraction is another powerful technique for studying crystal structures. Neutrons, like electrons, can also be diffracted by crystals. However, unlike electrons, neutrons are not affected by the electric charge of the atoms in the crystal. This allows for the study of materials with low atomic numbers, such as hydrogen and helium.

Similar to electron diffraction, neutron diffraction can provide information about the crystal structure, including lattice constants and symmetries. It can also be used to study magnetic materials, as neutrons have a magnetic moment and can interact with the magnetic moments of atoms in the crystal.

In both electron and neutron diffraction, the diffraction pattern is a result of the interference of the scattered waves from the atoms in the crystal. This interference is dependent on the crystal structure and can be used to determine the arrangement of atoms in the crystal.

In conclusion, electron and neutron diffraction are powerful techniques for studying the structure of crystalline materials. They provide valuable information about the crystal structure, properties, and defects, making them essential tools for materials science and solid state physics. 


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.3 Electron and Neutron Diffraction:

### Subsection (optional): 19.3b Neutron Diffraction and its Applications

In the previous section, we discussed how electron diffraction can be used to determine the crystal structure of a material. However, electrons are not the only particles that can be used for diffraction experiments. In this section, we will explore how neutrons can also be used to study crystal structures.

Neutron diffraction, also known as elastic neutron scattering, is a powerful technique for determining the atomic and magnetic structure of a material. Similar to X-ray diffraction, a sample is placed in a beam of neutrons and the resulting diffraction pattern provides information about the structure of the material. However, neutrons and X-rays provide complementary information due to their different scattering properties. While X-rays are better suited for superficial analysis, neutrons have a higher penetration depth and are better suited for bulk samples.

The technique requires a source of neutrons, which are usually produced in a nuclear reactor or spallation source. At a research reactor, a crystal monochromator is used to select the desired neutron wavelength, while at a spallation source, the time of flight technique is used to filter neutron pulses with the desired wavelength. The setup may also include movable components to adjust the neutron beam.

Neutron diffraction is most commonly performed as powder diffraction, which only requires a polycrystalline powder sample. However, single crystal work is also possible, although the crystals must be much larger than those used in single-crystal X-ray crystallography. Typically, crystals of about 1 mm$^3$ are used.

One of the main advantages of neutron diffraction is its ability to provide information about the magnetic structure of a material. Neutrons have a magnetic moment, which allows them to interact with the magnetic moments of atoms in a material. This makes neutron diffraction a valuable tool for studying magnetic materials and their properties.

In addition to studying crystal structures, neutron diffraction can also be used for other applications such as studying phase transitions, determining the location of hydrogen atoms in a crystal, and analyzing the structure of complex materials such as proteins and polymers.

In conclusion, neutron diffraction is a powerful technique for studying the structure of materials, providing complementary information to X-ray diffraction. Its ability to provide information about magnetic structures and its various applications make it a valuable tool in the field of solid state physics. 


# Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter 19: Crystallography and Diffraction:

### Section: 19.3 Electron and Neutron Diffraction:

### Subsection (optional): 19.3c Comparison of X-ray, Electron and Neutron Diffraction

In the previous sections, we discussed how X-ray and electron diffraction can be used to determine the crystal structure of a material. However, these are not the only techniques available for studying crystal structures. In this section, we will compare and contrast X-ray, electron, and neutron diffraction, and discuss their respective strengths and limitations.

X-ray diffraction is the most commonly used technique for crystal structure determination. This is due to the high penetration depth of X-rays, which allows for the analysis of both surface and bulk samples. Additionally, X-rays have a high scattering power, making them ideal for studying materials with low atomic numbers. However, X-rays are not sensitive to magnetic structures, and their high energy can cause radiation damage to the sample.

Electron diffraction, on the other hand, is better suited for studying materials with high atomic numbers. This is because electrons have a shorter wavelength compared to X-rays, allowing for higher resolution in the diffraction pattern. Additionally, electrons can provide information about the magnetic structure of a material. However, electron diffraction is limited to thin samples due to the low penetration depth of electrons.

Neutron diffraction, as we discussed in the previous section, is a powerful technique for studying both the atomic and magnetic structure of a material. Neutrons have a high penetration depth, making them ideal for studying bulk samples. They are also sensitive to magnetic structures, making them a valuable tool for studying magnetic materials. However, neutron diffraction requires specialized facilities and equipment, making it less accessible compared to X-ray and electron diffraction.

In terms of instrumental and sample requirements, X-ray diffraction is the most straightforward technique. It only requires a source of X-rays and a sample holder. Electron diffraction requires a high-energy electron beam and a specialized microscope for analyzing the diffraction pattern. Neutron diffraction, as mentioned earlier, requires a source of neutrons and specialized equipment for filtering and selecting the desired neutron wavelength.

In conclusion, each diffraction technique has its own strengths and limitations. X-ray diffraction is the most commonly used technique due to its high penetration depth and scattering power. Electron diffraction is better suited for studying materials with high atomic numbers and can provide information about magnetic structures. Neutron diffraction is a powerful tool for studying both atomic and magnetic structures, but it requires specialized facilities and equipment. By understanding the differences between these techniques, researchers can choose the most appropriate method for their specific research needs.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of crystallography and diffraction. We have learned about the different types of crystal structures, including the simple cubic, body-centered cubic, and face-centered cubic structures. We have also discussed the importance of symmetry in crystal structures and how it can be used to classify different types of crystals. Additionally, we have delved into the principles of diffraction and how it can be used to study the atomic arrangement in crystals. By understanding the principles of crystallography and diffraction, we can gain a deeper understanding of the properties and behavior of solids.

## Chapter: Textbook for Theory of Solids I

### Introduction

Welcome to the twentieth chapter of "Textbook for Theory of Solids I"! In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of solid state physics. This branch of physics deals with the study of the physical properties of solid materials, such as metals, semiconductors, and insulators. Solid state physics is a crucial field of study as it helps us understand the behavior of materials at the atomic and molecular level, which in turn allows us to develop new technologies and improve existing ones.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to solid state physics, including crystal structures, lattice vibrations, electronic band structures, and semiconductor devices. We will explore the fundamental principles and theories that govern the behavior of solids, and how they can be applied to real-world applications. Through this chapter, we hope to provide you with a solid foundation in solid state physics and inspire you to further explore this exciting field of study.

So, let's dive into the world of solid state physics and discover the wonders of the microscopic world that make up the materials we interact with every day. Get ready to expand your knowledge and understanding of the physical world around us!


### Section: 20.1 Electrical Properties of Solids:

Solid state physics is a branch of physics that deals with the study of the physical properties of solid materials. In this section, we will focus on the electrical properties of solids, specifically electrical conductivity and resistivity.

Electrical conductivity is a measure of how easily a material allows the flow of electric current. It is denoted by the symbol $\sigma$ and is the inverse of resistivity, denoted by the symbol $\rho$. The unit of electrical conductivity is siemens per meter (S/m) and the unit of resistivity is ohm-meter ($\Omega$m).

The electrical conductivity of a material is determined by its electronic band structure. In metals, the valence electrons are delocalized and can easily move through the material, resulting in high electrical conductivity. In contrast, in insulators, the valence electrons are tightly bound to the atoms and cannot move freely, resulting in low electrical conductivity. Semiconductors fall in between metals and insulators, with moderate electrical conductivity due to the presence of a band gap that allows some electrons to move through the material.

The electrical resistivity of a material is affected by various factors such as temperature, impurities, and defects in the crystal structure. As temperature increases, the resistivity of a material also increases due to increased thermal vibrations that impede the flow of electrons. Impurities and defects in the crystal structure can also disrupt the flow of electrons, resulting in higher resistivity.

To measure the electrical resistivity of a material, we use a four-probe method. This method involves inserting four metal probes into the material, with two probes used to introduce a current and the other two probes used to measure the voltage. By measuring the voltage and current, we can calculate the resistivity of the material.

One commonly used instrument for measuring electrical resistivity is the Wenner array, which consists of four probes arranged in a linear array. However, this array has a wide span for its depth of investigation, leading to problems with horizontal resolution. To overcome this, the twin-probe array was developed, which has become the standard for archaeological use. This array has four probes, with one current and one voltage probe mounted on a mobile frame and the other two probes placed remotely. This configuration allows for a more compact array and superior horizontal resolution.

In recent years, wheeled arrays have also been developed to increase the rate of survey. These arrays use spiked wheels or metal disks to quickly move the probes across the material, resulting in a faster survey rate.

In conclusion, the electrical properties of solids play a crucial role in determining the behavior and applications of materials. By understanding the electronic band structure and factors that affect electrical conductivity and resistivity, we can gain a deeper understanding of the physical properties of solids. 


### Section: 20.1 Electrical Properties of Solids:

Solid state physics is a branch of physics that deals with the study of the physical properties of solid materials. In this section, we will focus on the electrical properties of solids, specifically electrical conductivity and resistivity.

Electrical conductivity is a measure of how easily a material allows the flow of electric current. It is denoted by the symbol $\sigma$ and is the inverse of resistivity, denoted by the symbol $\rho$. The unit of electrical conductivity is siemens per meter (S/m) and the unit of resistivity is ohm-meter ($\Omega$m).

The electrical conductivity of a material is determined by its electronic band structure. In metals, the valence electrons are delocalized and can easily move through the material, resulting in high electrical conductivity. In contrast, in insulators, the valence electrons are tightly bound to the atoms and cannot move freely, resulting in low electrical conductivity. Semiconductors fall in between metals and insulators, with moderate electrical conductivity due to the presence of a band gap that allows some electrons to move through the material.

In this section, we will focus on two types of semiconductors: III-V semiconductors and II-VI semiconductors. III-V semiconductors, such as BN, BP, and GaN, have a crystal structure where the atoms of group III elements (such as boron, aluminum, and gallium) are bonded to atoms of group V elements (such as nitrogen, phosphorus, and arsenic). II-VI semiconductors, such as CdS, CdTe, and ZnO, have a crystal structure where the atoms of group II elements (such as cadmium and zinc) are bonded to atoms of group VI elements (such as sulfur, selenium, and tellurium).

The electrical resistivity of a material is affected by various factors such as temperature, impurities, and defects in the crystal structure. As temperature increases, the resistivity of a material also increases due to increased thermal vibrations that impede the flow of electrons. Impurities and defects in the crystal structure can also disrupt the flow of electrons, resulting in higher resistivity.

To measure the electrical resistivity of a material, we use a four-probe method. This method involves inserting four metal probes into the material, with two probes used to introduce a current and the other two probes used to measure the voltage. By measuring the voltage and current, we can calculate the resistivity of the material.

One commonly used instrument for measuring electrical resistivity is the Wenner array, which consists of four probes arranged in a straight line. The two outer probes are used to introduce a current, while the two inner probes measure the voltage. The distance between the probes is varied to obtain multiple measurements and calculate the resistivity of the material.

In addition to semiconductors, other types of materials also exhibit interesting electrical properties. Metal oxides, such as TiO<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and Cu<sub>2</sub>O, have been extensively studied for their potential applications in photoelectrochemistry. These materials have a wide range of band gaps and can be tuned to have different electrical properties.

Organic dyes, such as methylene blue, have also been studied for their electrical properties. These dyes have a conjugated structure that allows for the delocalization of electrons, resulting in high electrical conductivity. They have potential applications in organic electronics and solar cells.

In conclusion, the electrical properties of solids are determined by their electronic band structure and can be affected by various factors such as temperature, impurities, and defects. Semiconductors, metal oxides, and organic dyes are all examples of materials with interesting electrical properties that have potential applications in various fields. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specific properties of semiconductors and insulators.


### Section: 20.1 Electrical Properties of Solids:

Solid state physics is a branch of physics that deals with the study of the physical properties of solid materials. In this section, we will focus on the electrical properties of solids, specifically electrical conductivity and resistivity.

Electrical conductivity is a measure of how easily a material allows the flow of electric current. It is denoted by the symbol $\sigma$ and is the inverse of resistivity, denoted by the symbol $\rho$. The unit of electrical conductivity is siemens per meter (S/m) and the unit of resistivity is ohm-meter ($\Omega$m).

The electrical conductivity of a material is determined by its electronic band structure. In metals, the valence electrons are delocalized and can easily move through the material, resulting in high electrical conductivity. In contrast, in insulators, the valence electrons are tightly bound to the atoms and cannot move freely, resulting in low electrical conductivity. Semiconductors fall in between metals and insulators, with moderate electrical conductivity due to the presence of a band gap that allows some electrons to move through the material.

In this section, we will focus on two types of semiconductors: III-V semiconductors and II-VI semiconductors. III-V semiconductors, such as BN, BP, and GaN, have a crystal structure where the atoms of group III elements (such as boron, aluminum, and gallium) are bonded to atoms of group V elements (such as nitrogen, phosphorus, and arsenic). II-VI semiconductors, such as CdS, CdTe, and ZnO, have a crystal structure where the atoms of group II elements (such as cadmium and zinc) are bonded to atoms of group VI elements (such as sulfur, selenium, and tellurium).

The electrical resistivity of a material is affected by various factors such as temperature, impurities, and defects in the crystal structure. As temperature increases, the resistivity of a material also increases due to increased thermal vibrations that impede the movement of electrons. This phenomenon is known as thermal resistivity and is described by the Wiedemann-Franz law, which states that the ratio of thermal conductivity to electrical conductivity is proportional to temperature.

Impurities in a material can also affect its electrical resistivity. When impurities are introduced into a material, they can disrupt the regular crystal structure and create defects. These defects can act as scattering centers for electrons, hindering their movement and increasing the resistivity of the material. This is known as impurity resistivity.

In addition to thermal and impurity resistivity, there are also other types of resistivity that can affect the electrical properties of solids. These include lattice resistivity, which is caused by the vibrations of the crystal lattice, and grain boundary resistivity, which is caused by the boundaries between different grains in a polycrystalline material.

One of the most interesting phenomena related to electrical properties of solids is superconductivity. Superconductivity is a state in which a material exhibits zero electrical resistance and expels magnetic fields. This phenomenon was first discovered in metals, but has since been observed in a variety of materials, including semiconductors.

Under an electric field, monolayers of molybdenum disulfide (MoS$_2$) have been found to exhibit superconductivity at temperatures below 9.4 K, as reported by Marvin L. Cohen. This discovery has opened up new possibilities for the study and application of superconductivity in low-dimensional materials.

In conclusion, the electrical properties of solids are determined by various factors such as electronic band structure, temperature, and impurities. Understanding these properties is crucial for the development of new materials and technologies. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the topic of superconductivity and its applications in solid state physics.


# Title: Textbook for Theory of Solids I":

## Chapter: - Chapter 20: Solid State Physics:

### Section: - Section: 20.2 Magnetic Properties of Solids:

### Subsection (optional): 20.2a Diamagnetism and Paramagnetism

In the previous section, we discussed the electrical properties of solids, specifically electrical conductivity and resistivity. In this section, we will shift our focus to the magnetic properties of solids, specifically diamagnetism and paramagnetism.

Magnetism is a fundamental property of matter that arises from the spin and orbital motion of electrons. In solids, the magnetic properties are determined by the electronic structure and the interactions between the electrons. There are three types of magnetism observed in solids: diamagnetism, paramagnetism, and ferromagnetism.

Diamagnetism is a weak form of magnetism that is exhibited by all materials. It arises from the orbital motion of electrons in atoms and molecules, which creates a magnetic moment that opposes an applied magnetic field. This results in a slight repulsion of the material from the magnetic field. Diamagnetism is a universal property of matter and is present in all materials, but it is usually overshadowed by other forms of magnetism.

Paramagnetism, on the other hand, is a stronger form of magnetism that is exhibited by materials with unpaired electrons. In these materials, the unpaired electrons have a magnetic moment that aligns with an applied magnetic field, resulting in a net attraction to the field. The strength of paramagnetism is directly proportional to the number of unpaired electrons in the material.

Examples of paramagnetic materials include transition metals, rare earth elements, and some compounds such as iron oxide. These materials exhibit paramagnetism due to the presence of unpaired electrons in their electronic structure. However, the strength of paramagnetism is also affected by the interactions between the electrons, which can either enhance or suppress the magnetic moment.

In systems with minimal interactions, such as a dilute gas of monatomic hydrogen atoms, the only pure paramagnet is observed. Each atom has one non-interacting unpaired electron, resulting in a strong paramagnetic response. However, in most materials, the interactions between the electrons play a significant role in determining the magnetic properties.

In contrast to paramagnetism, ferromagnetism is a much stronger form of magnetism that arises from the alignment of magnetic moments in a material. This alignment can occur spontaneously, resulting in a permanent magnet. Ferromagnetism is observed in materials such as iron, nickel, and cobalt, which have a high concentration of unpaired electrons and strong interactions between them.

In the next subsection, we will focus on the Curie and Curie-Weiss laws, which describe the magnetic susceptibility of paramagnetic materials. We will also discuss the effects of temperature and impurities on the magnetic properties of solids.
